SHORT COMMUNICATION
Ultra-high-throughput microbial community analysis
on the Illumina HiSeq and MiSeq platforms
J Gregory Caporaso1, Christian L Lauber2, William A Walters3, Donna Berg-Lyons2,
James Huntley4, Noah Fierer2,5, Sarah M Owens6, Jason Betley7, Louise Fraser7,
Markus Bauer7, Niall Gormley7, Jack A Gilbert6,8, Geoff Smith7and Rob Knight9,10
1Department of Computer Science, Northern Arizona University, Flagstaff, AZ, USA;2Cooperative Institute
for Research in Environmental Sciences, UCB 216, University of Colorado, Boulder, CO, USA;3Department
of Molecular, Cellular and Developmental Biology, UCB 347, University of Colorado, Boulder, CO, USA;
4Colorado Initiative in Molecular Biotechnology, UCB 347, University of Colorado, Boulder, CO, USA;
5Department of Ecology and Evolutionary Biology, UCB 334, University of Colorado, Boulder, Colorado, USA;
6Argonne National Laboratory, Argonne, IL, USA;7Illumina Cambridge Ltd., Chesterford Research Park,
Saffron Walden, Essex, UK;8Department of Ecology and Evolution, University of Chicago, Chicago, IL, USA;
9Department of Chemistry and Biochemistry, UCB 215, University of Colorado, Boulder, CO, USA and
10Howard Hughes Medical Institute, University of Colorado at Boulder, UCB 215, Boulder, CO, USA
DNA sequencing continues to decrease in cost with the Illumina HiSeq2000 generating up to 600 Gb
of paired-end 100 base reads in a ten-day run. Here we present a protocol for community amplicon
sequencing on the HiSeq2000 and MiSeq Illumina platforms, and apply that protocol to sequence 24microbial communities from host-associated and free-living environments. A critical question asmore sequencing platforms become available is whether biological conclusions derived on one
platform are consistent with what would be derived on a different platform. We show that the
protocol developed for these instruments successfully recaptures known biological results, andadditionally that biological conclusions are consistent across sequencing platforms (the HiSeq2000
versus the MiSeq) and across the sequenced regions of amplicons.
The ISME Journal (2012) 6,1621–1624; doi:10.1038/ismej.2012.8; published online 8 March 2012
Subject Category:
microbial ecology and functional diversity of natural habitats
Keywords: illumine; barcoded sequencing; QIIME
DNA sequencing cost continues to decline: a vast
price per sequence decrease on Illumina HiSeq2000
and MiSeq platforms further supports democratiza-tion of sequencing (Tringe and Hugenholtz, 2008).
Interest in amplicon sequencing on Illumina is
growing (Bartram et al ., 2011; Caporaso et al .,
2011; Zhou et al ., 2011), largely due to lower cost
per sequence than other platforms, enabling high-
throughput microbial ecology at the greatest cover-age yet possible. Although some technical issuesexist with community sequencing, such as PCR
primer biases and differential DNA extraction
efficiency from different organisms in complexcommunities, these techniques continue to vastly
expand our understanding of the microbial world.
Here we present an amplicon sequencing protocol
for the HiSeq2000 and MiSeq platforms, and applythis protocol to sequence host-associated and free-
living microbial communities to verify that biologi-
cal conclusions drawn from the data are consistentacross platforms and sequence reads. The HiSeq and
MiSeq platforms differ markedly in scale. The
HiSeq2000 produces 450 Gb per day, and in the
course of a 10.8 day run produces 1.6 billion
100-base paired-end reads. By contrast, the MiSeq
is for single-day experiments, and generates 1.5 Gbper day from 5 million 150-base paired-end reads.Our results capture known differences between
microbial communities on each platform; biological
conclusions drawn are consistent across platformsand sequence reads. This protocol is therefore ready
for widespread use in microbial community analy-
sis, such as by the Earth Microbiome Project (Gilbertet al ., 2010), which has adopted it for amplicon
sequencing. Details on the sequencing protocol are
provided as Supplementary Methods.
Twenty-four samples were sequenced on three
paired-end Illumina HiSeq2000 lanes, and in one
paired-end MiSeq run. The samples represented soil
(source: USA; n¼8) and several host-associated
environment types: human feces (source: USA;
Received 12 September 2011; revised 13 January 2012; accepted
19 January 2012; published online 8 March 2012Correspondence: R Knight, Howard Hughes Medical Institute,University of Colorado at Boulder, UCB 215, Boulder, CO 80309,USA.E-mail: rob@spot.colorado.eduThe ISME Journal (2012) 6, 1621–1624
&2012 International Society for Microbial Ecology All rights reserved 1751-7362/12
www.nature.com/ismejn¼2), mouth (source: USA; n¼2) and skin (source:
USA; n¼6); canine feces (source: USA; n¼1)
mouth (source: USA; n¼1) and skin (source: USA;
n¼4). These four paired-end lanes (three on HiSeq
and one on MiSeq) resulted in eight sets of reads,
corresponding to 50and 30reads from each lane.
These sets of reads were treated as independentreplicates to assess the reproducibility of the results.
We were primarily interested in whether known
differences between microbial communities couldbe recaptured on these Illumina platforms to
determine their suitability for large-scale surveys
of microbial communities. We observed severalexpected results in principal coordinates plots ofweighted UniFrac distances (Figure 1). First, we
observed primary separation of samples based on
whether they were derived from a free-livingenvironment (soil; cyan) or host-associated environ-
ment (all other colors) (Ley et al ., 2008). Next we
observed separation of fecal samples (yellow; red)from all other host-associated sample types (Costello
et al., 2009).
We were additionally interested in reproducibility
across lanes and reads within and between each
platform. To test this, we ran the 24 samples on
three HiSeq paired-end lanes and 1 MiSeq paired-end lane, and analyzed each resulting set ofreads independently. As our biological conclusionsare frequently driven by the results of principal
coordinates analyses based on weighted UniFrac
distances, we compared these plots using Procrustes
analysis (Gower, 1975; Figure 1; Table 1) asimplemented in QIIME and found that the observa-
tions were highly reproducible across lanes, read
directions and platforms. All 28 possible lane/read pair combinations produced highly significant
P-values based on 10 000 Monte Carlo iterations
(Po0.0001; Bonferroni-adjusted a
0.01¼0.0004).
Taken together, these results suggest that the
protocol previously developed for high-throughput
community sequencing on the Illumina GAIIx hasbeen successfully adapted for the HiSeq2000 andMiSeq platforms, again greatly decreasing the cost
per sequence of amplicon sequencing to B15 000
single-end reads per USD$1 on the HiSeq2000. Forexample, based on our lowest high-quality sequence
per lane count of 22 928 291 reads (Supplementary
File 2, HiSeq 3
0lane 6), if using all 2167 barcodes in
each of 15 lanes on the HiSeq2000, leaving one lane
for a control, then it is possible to sequence 32 505
samples in a week at a depth of 10 580 sequences persample for approximately $22 000 in sequencing
costs. Longer barcodes could additionally be devel-
oped to facilitate more sequences per sample at alower depth of sequencing. On the basis of thelowest high-quality sequence count on the MiSeq of

using all 2167 barcodes, it is possible to sequence2167 samples in a 12 h run at a depth of 740

A relevant question is whether the decreased cost
of sequencing should be applied to obtain deeper
coverage of samples, or to increase the number ofsamples that are sequenced. Figure 1c compares the
results of sequencing the same samples on the HiSeq
2000 at a median depth of 1207 709 sequences persample and the MiSeq platform at a depth of 43 271sequences per sample. The highly significant Pro-
crustes result ( Po0.0001) implies that we draw the
same beta diversity conclusions from either sequen-cing run, despite a two order of magnitude increase
in sequencing depth on the HiSeq2000. Similarly,
when sampling to only 10 sequences per sampleProcrustes results are still highly significant
(Po0.0001; Supplementary Figure 1), although the
higher M
2value indicates that the correlation is not
as strong as when sampling to 100 sequences per
sample. These observations, in agreement with
studies that have addressed this question directly(Kuczynski et al., 2010), suggest that increasing the
sequencing depth is not likely to provide additional
insight into questions of beta diversity, and we
therefore argue that (for questions of beta diversityin particular) the decreased cost of sequencing
should be applied to study microbial systems using
many more samples, for example, in dense temporalor spatial analyses, rather than with many more
sequences per sample. Of course, if the objective is
to identify taxa that are very rare in communities,deeper sequencing will be advantageous. Addition-ally we note that while as few as 10 sequences persample may be useful for differentiating very
different environment types (for example, soil andfeces), as environments become more similar (for
example, two soil samples of different pH) more
sequences will be required to differentiate them.
As sequencing costs continue to decrease our
studies of the microbial world can continue to
increase in scope. The protocol presented hereopens the HiSeq2000 and MiSeq Illumina platforms
to community amplicon sequencing. The data
generated by each is similar, but differs in scaleand therefore support different applications. Forlarge projects where time is less of an issue but cost
per sequence is a major concern, the HiSeq platform
allows massively parallel sequencing at the lowestcost. Here we show that comparable data can be
generated on the MiSeq for smaller projects where it
is important to process samples quickly, for exam-ple, in routine environmental or patient monitoring
or in preliminary investigations for larger projects.
We expect that this is another step toward the era ofubiquitous DNA sequencing, when sequencers
become standard equipment in research and clinical
laboratories. Finally, we show that technical repli-cates run on different sequencing platforms andfrom sequencing of different regions of amplicons
should yield the same biological conclusions:
critical information as more sequencing platformsbecome available.

 MiSeq	  platforms	  2	  Supplementary	  Methods	  3	  PCR	  and	  sequencing	  were	  performed	  using	  a	  modified	  version	  of	  the	  protocol	  4	  presented	  in	  (Caporaso	  et	  al	  2010b),	  adapted	  for	  the	  Illumina	  HiSeq2000	  and	  MiSeq.	  5	  Briefly,	  the	  V4	  region	  of	  the	  16S	  rRNA	  gene	  was	  amplified	  with	  region-­‐specific	  6	  primers	  that	  included	  the	  Illumina	  flowcell	  adapter	  sequences.	  	  The	  reverse	  7	  amplification	  primer	  also	  contained	  a	  twelve	  base	  barcode	  sequence	  that	  supports	  8	  pooling	  of	  up	  to	  2,167	  different	  samples	  in	  each	  lane.	  	  After	  cluster	  formation	  on	  a	  9	  HiSeq	  or	  MiSeq	  instrument,	  the	  amplicons	  were	  sequenced	  with	  custom	  primers.	  10	  These	  sequencing	  primers	  were	  designed	  to	  be	  complimentary	  to	  the	  V4	  11	  amplification	  primers	  to	  avoid	  sequencing	  of	  the	  primers,	  and	  the	  barcode	  is	  read	  12	  using	  a	  third	  sequencing	  primer	  in	  an	  additional	  cycle.	  The	  amplification	  primers	  13	  were	  adapted	  from	  the	  (Caporaso,	  et	  al.,	  2010)	  protocol	  to	  include	  nine	  extra	  bases	  14	  in	  the	  adapter	  region	  of	  the	  forward	  amplification	  primer	  that	  support	  paired-­‐end	  15	  sequencing	  on	  the	  HiSeq/MiSeq.	  The	  amplification	  and	  sequencing	  primers	  16	  additionally	  contain	  a	  new	  pad	  region	  to	  avoid	  primer-­‐dimer	  formation	  with	  the	  17	  modified	  adapter.	  The	  primer	  sequences,	  including	  the	  2,167	  valid,	  secondary-­‐18	  structure	  checked	  Golay-­‐barcoded	  reverse	  primers,	  are	  provided	  in	  Supplementary	  19	  File	  1.	  	  20	  Quality	  filtering	  of	  reads	  was	  applied	  as	  described	  previously	  (Caporaso	  et	  al	  21	  2010b).	  Reads	  were	  truncated	  at	  their	  first	  low-­‐quality	  base	  (defined	  by	  an	  ‘A’	  or	  ‘B’	  22	  quality	  score).	  Reads	  shorter	  than	  75	  bases	  were	  then	  discarded,	  as	  were	  reads	  23	  whose	  barcode	  did	  not	  match	  an	  expected	  barcode.	  Sequence	  counts	  before	  and	  24	  after	  quality	  filtering	  are	  provided	  in	  Supplementary	  File	  2.	  In	  addition	  to	  the	  25	  twenty-­‐four	  experimental	  samples,	  the	  MiSeq	  run	  also	  contained	  a	  control	  library	  26	  made	  from	  phiX174	  which,	  in	  this	  run,	  accounted	  for	  47%	  of	  reads.	  	  The	  phiX	  27	  control	  was	  used	  because	  of	  the	  limited	  sequence	  diversity	  among	  the	  16S	  28	  amplicons.	  29	  Reads	  were	  assigned	  to	  OTUs	  using	  a	  closed-­‐reference	  OTU	  picking	  protocol	  using	  30	  the	  QIIME	  toolkit	  (Caporaso	  et	  al	  2010a),	  where	  uclust	  (Edgar	  2010)	  was	  applied	  to	  31	  search	  sequences	  against	  a	  subset	  of	  the	  Greengenes	  database	  (DeSantis	  et	  al	  2006)	  32	  filtered	  at	  97%	  identity.	  Reads	  were	  assigned	  to	  OTUs	  based	  on	  their	  best	  hit	  to	  this	  33	  database	  at	  greater	  than	  or	  equal	  to	  97%	  sequence	  identity.	  Reads	  that	  did	  not	  34	  match	  a	  reference	  sequence	  were	  discarded.	  	  Median	  sequence	  counts	  per	  sample	  35	  after	  OTU	  picking	  were	  1,319,792,	  1,102,905,	  and	  1,126,194	  for	  the	  three	  HiSeq	  5’	  36	  read	  replicates;	  718,186,	  918,249,	  and	  1,011,406	  for	  the	  three	  HiSeq	  3’	  read	  37	  replicates;	  43,966	  for	  the	  MiSeq	  5’	  reads;	  and	  46,232	  for	  the	  MiSeq	  3’	  reads.	  38	  Taxonomy	  was	  assigned	  to	  each	  read	  by	  accepting	  the	  Greengenes	  taxonomy	  string	  39	  of	  the	  best	  matching	  Greengenes	  sequence.	  Weighted	  Unifrac	  distances	  were	  40	  	  2	  computed	  between	  all	  samples	  in	  each	  replicate,	  and	  principal	  coordinates	  analysis	  41	  was	  applied	  to	  visualize	  the	  results.	  	  42	  HiSeq	  preparation	  and	  sequencing	  protocol	  43	  16S	  library	  pools	  were	  initially	  analyzed	  by	  Bioanalyzer	  (Agilent	  Technologies,	  44	  using	  DNA1000	  chips)	  to	  ascertain	  library	  quality	  and	  average	  size	  distribution.	  	  45	  The	  concentration	  of	  the	  pools	  was	  determined	  via	  Qubit	  (Invitrogen,	  using	  High	  46	  Sensitivity	  reagents),	  and	  the	  pools	  diluted	  to	  2nM.	  	  Following	  NaOH	  denaturation,	  47	  the	  libraries	  were	  applied	  to	  a	  v2.5	  TruSeq	  Paired	  End	  HiSeq	  flow	  cell/cluster	  kit	  48	  (Illumina	  Inc.)	  at	  4pM	  per	  manufacturer’s	  instructions.	  	  For	  clustering,	  sequencing	  of	  49	  read	  1,	  sequencing	  of	  the	  index	  read	  and	  sequencing	  of	  read	  2,	  customsequencing	  50	  primers	  (IDT)	  were	  used	  at	  a	  final	  concentration	  of	  500nMin	  Illumina's	  51	  hybridization	  buffer	  (HT1).	  	  Sequencing	  on	  the	  HiSeq	  (v1	  SBS	  reagents)	  was	  done	  52	  according	  to	  manufacturer’s	  instructions.	  	  Application	  of	  the	  library	  pools	  resulted	  53	  in	  approximately	  340k	  clusters/mm2	  and	  38M	  reads	  pass-­‐filter.	  	  Base	  calling	  was	  54	  performed	  using	  CASAVA-­‐1.7.0	  (Illumina,	  Inc.).	  55	  MiSeq	  preparation	  and	  sequencing	  protocol	  56	  Quantify	  the	  library	  that	  is	  to	  be	  sequenced.	  Concentration	  should	  be	  recorded	  in	  57	  Molarity.	  The	  most	  accurate	  way	  to	  quantify	  the	  sample	  is	  by	  conducting	  qPCR.	  58	  Common,	  alternative	  methods	  include	  using	  the	  Agilent	  Bioanalyzer	  or	  the	  59	  Invitrogen	  Qubit.	  60	  Use	  the	  concentration	  determined	  to	  dilute	  the	  sample	  first	  to	  10nM,	  then	  to	  2nM	  in	  61	  a	  serial	  dilution.	  If	  the	  concentration	  of	  the	  amplicon	  pool	  is	  very	  high,	  it	  may	  be	  62	  necessary	  to	  take	  the	  sample	  through	  a	  more	  gradual	  serial	  dilution	  with	  a	  final	  goal	  63	  of	  2nM.	  64	  Once	  the	  sample	  has	  been	  brought	  down	  to	  2nM,	  the	  MiSeq	  Protocol	  provided	  by	  65	  Illumina	  should	  be	  followed	  for	  preparation	  of	  the	  library	  for	  sequencing.	  Once	  the	  66	  final	  desired	  concentration	  is	  reached,	  15-­‐30%	  denatured	  PhiX	  must	  be	  run	  with	  the	  67	  amplicon	  pool.	  Add	  the	  appropriate	  volumes	  of	  PhiX	  and	  amplicon	  pool	  to	  a	  68	  separate	  tube	  to	  maintain	  the	  appropriate	  concentration	  (Do	  not	  just	  spike	  in	  25ul	  69	  of	  PhiX	  into	  a	  1000ul	  tube	  of	  sample).	  This	  spike	  helps	  balance	  the	  extreme	  base	  70	  bias	  present	  in	  16S	  amplicon	  samples.	  Up	  to	  50%	  PhiX	  can	  be	  used	  to	  be	  extremely	  71	  conservative	  when	  sequencing	  samples	  that	  require	  fewer	  reads	  per	  index,	  or	  when	  72	  completing	  the	  first	  amplicon	  run	  to	  gauge	  the	  appropriate	  sample	  loading	  73	  concentration.	  	  74	  Sequencing	  on	  the	  MiSeq	  requires	  the	  use	  of	  aMiSeq	  Reagent	  Cartridge.	  When	  75	  sequencing	  custom	  16S	  rRNA	  amplicons,	  the	  300-­‐cycle	  PE	  kit	  is	  used.	  The	  reagent	  76	  cartridge	  must	  be	  thawed	  for	  about	  1-­‐1.5hrs	  before	  use.	  This	  should	  be	  thawed	  in	  a	  77	  bath	  of	  room	  temperature	  ultrapure	  water,	  no	  higher	  than	  the	  water	  line.	  Once	  the	  78	  cartridge	  has	  been	  thawed,	  place	  it	  in	  the	  4°C	  refrigerator.	  The	  box	  containing	  the	  79	  cartridge	  also	  contains	  Hybridization	  Buffer	  HT1,	  which	  should	  be	  thawed	  on	  the	  80	  benchtop,	  then	  placed	  in	  the	  4°C	  until	  use.	  81	  	  3	  The	  MiSeq	  uses	  a	  “Sample	  Sheet”	  .csv	  file	  set-­‐up	  through	  the	  Illumina	  Experiment	  82	  Manager	  on	  a	  separate	  PC	  to	  dictate	  the	  parameters	  of	  each	  run.	  These	  instructions	  83	  are	  based	  on	  version	  1.0.31	  of	  the	  Illumina	  Experiment	  Manager.	  When	  creating	  the	  84	  sample	  sheet	  for	  this	  run,	  under	  “Select	  Workflow”	  choose	  “MiSeq	  Reporter”	  and	  85	  then	  select	  “de	  novo	  Assembly”.(“Metagenomics”	  can	  also	  be	  selected	  at	  this	  stage.	  86	  The	  important	  point	  is	  to	  select	  “MiSeq	  Reporter”	  as	  this	  will	  allow	  you	  to	  obtain	  87	  sequences	  that	  are	  not	  demultiplexed,	  so	  demultiplexing	  can	  be	  performed	  with	  88	  QIIME.	  This	  is	  important	  because	  QIIME	  can	  correct	  barcode	  errors	  while	  the	  MiSeq	  89	  instrument	  software	  does	  not	  attempt	  to	  correct	  barcode	  errors.)	  Under	  the	  field	  90	  “Select	  Compatible	  Assay”	  select	  “TruSeq	  DNA/RNA”	  (see	  Screenshot	  1).	  	  Fill	  in	  the	  91	  number	  listed	  on	  the	  cartridge	  you’ll	  be	  using	  in	  the	  “Sample	  Sheet	  Name*”	  adding	  92	  two	  zeros	  before	  the	  300	  (e.g.	  MS0002657-­‐00300).	  Then,	  select	  “Paired	  End,”	  1	  93	  Index	  Read,	  Index	  Cycles	  6,	  and	  151x151bp	  (see	  Screenshot	  2).	  Note	  that	  despite	  the	  94	  barcodes	  being	  12	  bases,	  you	  should	  set	  Index	  Cycles	  to	  6	  in	  this	  step	  –	  this	  will	  be	  95	  corrected	  manually	  in	  a	  subsequent	  step.	  On	  the	  next	  screen	  (no	  screenshots),	  fill	  in	  96	  a	  Sample	  ID	  and	  select	  one	  of	  the	  standard	  barcodes	  Illumina	  provides	  (e.g.	  A001).	  	  97	  Once	  the	  required	  columns	  have	  been	  filled-­‐in,	  click	  in	  any	  box	  to	  see	  the	  word	  98	  “valid”	  in	  green	  and	  then	  proceed.	  99	  Once	  this	  .csv	  file	  is	  created,	  it	  will	  need	  to	  be	  edited	  manually	  to	  instruct	  the	  MiSeq	  100	  to	  conduct	  a	  12bp	  index	  read.	  This	  is	  achieved	  by	  opening	  the	  appropriate	  sample	  101	  sheet	  for	  the	  run	  in	  a	  text	  editor	  (e.g.	  Notepad	  on	  Windows,	  TextEdit	  on	  Mac,	  or	  102	  gedit	  on	  Linux).	  The	  columns	  in	  this	  sheet	  are	  comma	  separated,	  so	  it	  is	  crucial	  to	  103	  include/remove	  the	  appropriate	  amount	  of	  commas	  when	  editing	  the	  file.	  On	  the	  104	  line	  directly	  under	  [Settings]	  include	  the	  command	  OnlyGenerateFASTQ,1.	  105	  Next,	  under	  [Data]	  replace	  the	  6bp	  barcode	  with	  a	  12bp	  barcode	  (any	  will	  do)	  to	  106	  indicate	  to	  the	  instrument	  you	  want	  a	  12bp	  index	  read.	  You	  will	  also	  need	  to	  remove	  107	  the	  field	  17_Index_ID,by	  removing	  both	  the	  column	  name	  and	  comma.	  In	  order	  to	  108	  check	  that	  the	  columns	  appropriately	  align	  in	  your	  edited	  .csv,	  open	  the	  Sample	  109	  Sheet	  in	  Excel.	  In	  Excel,	  you	  should	  see	  that	  the	  columns	  line-­‐up	  and	  that	  the	  column	  110	  containing	  I7_Index_ID	  is	  gone	  (see	  Screenshot	  3,	  which	  is	  an	  example	  of	  what	  111	  the	  csv	  should	  look	  like	  after	  you’ve	  edited	  it).	  112	  When	  preparing	  the	  sequencing	  cartridge,	  pierce	  the	  foil	  with	  a	  1000ul	  pipette	  tip	  113	  and	  add	  600ul	  of	  the	  denatured	  library	  plus	  PhiX	  in	  the	  “Load	  Sample”	  well.	  Next,	  114	  pierce	  the	  foil	  seals	  with	  a	  pipette	  tip	  and	  add	  3.4ul	  of	  Index	  Sequencing	  Primer	  115	  (100uM)	  to	  reservoir	  13,	  3.4ul	  of	  Read	  1	  Sequencing	  Primer	  (100uM)	  into	  reservoir	  116	  12,	  and	  3.4ul	  of	  Read	  2	  Sequencing	  Primer	  (100um)	  to	  reservoir	  14.	  Next,	  mix	  the	  117	  contents	  of	  each	  of	  the	  reservoirs	  (12,	  13,	  and	  14)	  with	  a	  Pasteur	  pipette	  to	  ensure	  118	  that	  the	  primers	  added	  by	  the	  user	  are	  mixed	  with	  the	  standard	  Illumina	  cocktail	  119	  already	  in	  the	  reservoirs.	  Note	  that	  you	  should	  mix	  the	  contents	  of	  each	  reservoir	  120	  with	  itself	  so	  each	  internal	  mixture	  is	  homogenous	  -­‐	  do	  not	  mix	  the	  contents	  of	  121	  different	  reservoirs	  with	  one	  another.	  122	  After	  loading	  the	  flow	  cell,	  buffer,	  and	  MiSeq	  cartridge,	  the	  machine	  will	  search	  for	  123	  the	  sample	  sheet	  based	  on	  the	  barcode	  on	  the	  cartridge.	  If	  edited	  properly,	  the	  124	  	  4	  MiSeq	  will	  have	  no	  trouble	  accessing	  your	  Sample	  Sheet,	  and	  will	  indicate	  that	  the	  125	  run	  is	  314	  cycles	  (151bp	  x	  12bp	  x	  151bp).	  The	  machine	  will	  throw	  an	  error	  that	  this	  126	  run	  requires	  more	  cycles	  than	  the	  300	  cycle	  kit	  can	  complete,	  and	  that	  there	  could	  127	  be	  sequence	  quality	  issues.	  Ignore	  the	  error	  and	  continue	  with	  the	  sequencing	  run.	  128	  There	  are	  enough	  reagents	  in	  the	  cartridge	  for	  323	  cycles.	  	  129	  As	  of	  this	  writing	  the	  current	  version	  of	  the	  MiSeq	  Control	  Software	  (MCS)	  is	  RTA	  130	  1.13.0.	  RTA1.14	  is	  currently	  in	  development,	  and	  will	  provide	  better	  handling	  of	  131	  high-­‐density	  data.	  If	  you’re	  interested	  in	  using	  RTA1.14	  before	  it	  is	  released	  you	  132	  should	  contact	  Illumina	  for	  a	  software	  patch	  and	  instructions	  for	  installing	  it.	  133	  	  134	  	  135	  
	  136	  Screenshot	  1:	  Worflow	  selection.	  137	  	  5	  
	  138	  Screenshot	  2:	  Worflow	  parameters.	  139	  	  140	  	  6	  
	  141	  Screenshot	  3:	  Example	  csv	  file	  after	  editing.	  142	  	  143	  References	  144	  Caporaso	  JG,	  Kuczynski	  J,	  Stombaugh	  J,	  Bittinger	  K,	  Bushman	  FD,	  Costello	  EK	  et	  al	  145	  (2010a).	  QIIME	  allows	  analysis	  of	  high-­‐throughput	  community	  sequencing	  data.	  Nat	  146	  Methods7:	  335-­‐336.	  147	  	  148	  Caporaso	  JG,	  Lauber	  CL,	  Walters	  WA,	  Berg-­‐Lyons	  D,	  Lozupone	  CA,	  Turnbaugh	  PJ	  et	  al	  149	  (2010b).	  Global	  patterns	  of	  16S	  rRNA	  diversity	  at	  a	  depth	  of	  millions	  of	  sequences	  150	  per	  sample.	  Proc	  Natl	  Acad	  Sci	  U	  S	  A.	  151	  	  152	  DeSantis	  TZ,	  Hugenholtz	  P,	  Larsen	  N,	  Rojas	  M,	  Brodie	  EL,	  Keller	  K	  et	  al	  (2006).	  153	  Greengenes,	  a	  chimera-­‐checked	  16S	  rRNA	  gene	  database	  and	  workbench	  compatible	  154	  with	  ARB.	  Appl	  Environ	  Microbiol72:	  5069-­‐-­‐5072.	  155	  	  156	  Edgar	  RC	  (2010).	  Search	  and	  clustering	  orders	  of	  magnitude	  faster	  than	  BLAST.	  157	  Bioinformatics26:	  2460-­‐2461.	  158	  	  159
